=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.6
Two Player Value = Fair, better with more players.

Three Player Value = Fair.


Smash Up is a simple competitive card game where players battle each other for points.

This could be one of the easiest games to learn by reading the rules. This makes the game great for all level of gamers. The rules are extremely minimalistic. The only real rules of the game are play two cards then draw two cards. Other then that, players follow the commands on the cards themselves.

The game is powered through card abilities. Each card played has an ability it triggers. Players use those abilities in combinations to effect the game in their favor.

Players use their cards of gain control of scoring bases. Each base is scored at a pre-determined time. The player in control of the scoring base at that time earns those points. The first player to a set number of points win.

What I like about Smash Up is that it does not take itself to seriously. It is a silly fun game and it remains that way while playing due to the light-heartedness of the game. That makes Smash Up fun to play.

The artwork on the cards is nice and the card stock is heavy. The theme is truly pasted on although is does work well for the game. This game could handle just about any theme and still work fine.

While the game is meant to be played quickly, the game can grind a bit and tends to slow down as players read all the cards in play to try to find a good combination. 


Pros - 
- Easy to learn
- Fun light-hearted play


Cons - 
- Game can slow down and grind


Overall Playability = 6.6

Design = 7
Strategy = 7
Luck = 7
Artwork & Components= 7
Use of Mechanics = 7
Time of Play = 8
Fun = 8
Innovation = 5
Analysis Paralysis = 5
Theme = 5


[b]-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------[/b]


Here I will discuss the Smash Up expansion [thing=134726][/thing].

Overall I like this expansion, although it brings absolutely no new mechanics to the base game.

In fact, this expansion changes nothing.  It only adds more playable factions to the game and a few more scoring bases.

While each faction brings different abilities, nothing is changed in the game overall.

With that, I do not view this expansion as good, nor bad.  I view this expansion as completely neutral.  However, I do like the new factions just for the variety and new possible combinations.

Due to the that, I think the original scoring of the game holds and does not change.

More factions with different abilities, but the game remains the same.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Smash Up is a great deckbuilding game with simple rules and without unnecessary rules-baggage some games of this genre bring with them.

Deckbuilding is as easy as choosing two factions and shuffling them together - hence the moniker "the shufflebuilding game". Most factions are synergistic with the other factions, but there are some obvious dominant combinations (e.g. Aliens/Zombies) which are pretty strong with almost no downsides.

While I love Smash Up for it's simplicity, clever system and card design, the base scoring mechanic is what keeps this from being truly great: there is simply too much (boring) mental arithmetic involved for each player at every turn. Turns are usually dragging on for much longer than needed because players try to think of great combos to play, then count points, adjust the combo, count number again.. and so on. Score cards alleviate this issue, but it's still inelegant and requires more components. 

Smash Up certainly scratches the Magic: The Gathering itch without requiring a partner who has expert deckbuilding/metagame knowledge.

RATING: 5.5
Full review [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/1123645/smash-up-a-review-for-two-players-from-a-board-gam/]here.[/url]

The mechanics are written down on the cards, so unless you study each deck beforehand, you can throw strategy out the window. For a game that claims to be fun and quick, it was neither for us. Lots of reading, hand management, and manual calculating. 

This game would benefit from a "chit" system similar to Sentinels of the Multiverse, where all the modifiers could be placed out on the table for all to see. Instead, you must keep a running tally in your head and calculate each faction's power on the fly. This "keeping score in your head" is a major flaw, and the reason I've traded this game. I place this "mechanic" in contrast with games where the final score is unknown until the end, whilst one may make educated guesses along the way. 

I understand its appeal for those who enjoy it, it just wasn't for us.

UPDATE: Successfully traded for Mr. Jack in New York.

It doesn't happen every game, but it happens often enough to make me hesitate to suggest playing it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Played one 3P and one 4P game. Smash Up starts off with a geek-friendly premise of mashing several pop culture tropes together. The artwork is top-notch and the factions feel thematic in their execution, with zombies returning from the discard pile and ninjas slipping in at the last minute. 

Unfortunately, the game is bogged down by the need to track the effects of several cards and constantly recalculate whether bases have been captured. The mercurial nature of the game also does not gel well with the oodles of card text, and the game can drag as players often have to reread card effects when their turn arrives. Familiarity with the cards helps a little, but can only go so far. Finally, some combinations are clearly stronger than others. As it stands, Smash Up with 3 players is tolerable; I would actively avoid it with 4 players as its drawbacks are simply augmented.

RATING: 6
I love the concept. Ninja zombies or robot aliens? Sign me up.I also love the art - beautifully illustrated and very thematic.

However I am struggling to love the game itself as I find too many aspects of the game more tedious than fun. The biggest drag by far - the constant reading of text on each card. Not only will you spend a lot of time reading the cards in your hand, everyone needs to read every card played by any other player as well. Plus the need to constantly re-read all the base cards every now and then plus minion cards around bases plus base or minion modifiers on top of them. This is in stark contrast to games like Race for the Galaxy or 7 Wonders, where you just take a quick glance at the iconography on a card and instantly know what it does.

Then comes the constant need to calculate and recalculate the total power of cards around a base or even every single card, if some modifiers happen to be included. I have now printed out some custom basemats with a power track and +1/-1 power tokens for individual cards, which will hopefully mitigate the problem, but without these, the constant book-keeping was sucking out most of the fun from the game.

Additionally there is nothing provided in the box to keep score with. We used some random cards from another game as victory points and I have now printed out a custom VP track to use in the game but really - is it necessary to keep costs down to a level where vital parts of the game needs to custom-printed at home?

My initial rating of 6 is based on the game as it ships without the basemats, power tokens and score track. If the custom additions happen to diminish the upkeep overhead and make the game more enjoyable, I may increase the rating but otherwise - I will most likely put it up for sale.

EDIT: the custom basemats and victory tracks diminished the cognitive overhead needed to keep track of base powers and victory points, but game enjoyment unfortunately did not increase. I generally dislike games, where knowledge of the deck can seriously impact your success in the game and unfortunately "Smash Up" is one of those games.

RATING: 5.4
Components - .9/1 Cards box and artwork are great. The rules are simple, and that is actually where it gets its knock. There were a lot of situations where we felt the cards weren't specific, and the rules being a little more flushed out would have helped.

Fun - .3/1 This game is a big meh to me. Everyone else seemed to enjoy it, but honestly it's like a really bad version of a deck builder. There's all downsides of a deck builder (large down times for people who have large chaining decks) but without any of the strategy. You either have decks that support chaining or you don't, and since your deck is set at the beginning, you're stuck with whatever you draw. In addition there is a lot of tedium in maintenance. Every turn you're spending time adding values for multiple bases. If you don't know the cards, you also are reading them all because they be modifying the values. This equals a lot of downtime which isn't fun.

Replayability - .7/1 There's 8 decks, meaning there's a fair amount of combinations out of the core set. Then again, the same is likely to feel a little samey after repeated plays.

Strategic - .5/1 The biggest success/fail boils down to what you draw. As mentioned before unlike a deckbuilder where you are controlling the growth of your deck, this gives you everything at the beginning. With that you're purely stuck with what you get at the draw. If you happen to never draw any minions, you're going to be having a lot of short turns.

Theme - .5/1 The combinations of races sounds great. The problem is that during play, it doesn't really come through that the races are together. I'm not sure how, but it feels like there could have been something to more strongly tie them together.


Personal Rating - 2.5/5 As I've mentioned, the game falls flat. I believe that if you played enough to memorize all the different card's abilities, you likely could cut out a fair amount of the downtime, but the problem is that I'm not sure there is enough there to warrant investing that kind of time. In addition, if you ever played with someone who was new, you'd have to go through that pain with them. The game feels like there's a lot of potential with expansions, but I who wants to spend that kind of additional money on a mediocre game in the hopes that it gets better? Basically my feelings can be summed up that the game plays out like a deckbuilder without the deck building build up of your engine. You don't really have any control on the cards you get/have and games rarely have you getting through your whole deck (so your play is dictated quite a lot by how your original shuffle went).

RATING: 8.5
Cool deckbuilding cardgame. Easy to pick up.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
1 play, 4er, to 8VP (GenCon demo)

[b]--- Game Summary ---[/b]
The game comes with 8 decks (ninjas, zombies, robots, aliens, etc.) which each excel at one type of thing. Players select (switchback style) 2 of these and create their own deck by shuffling them together. Draw a hand of 5. There is also a deck of bases: place n+1 face up on table. Bases show a minimum total to resolve, VP rewards for 1st-3rd places, and some text that triggers when it's resolved or during the game.

On your turn, you can play some cards for instant effects. Then, may play one minion and/or one action (any order). Minions are played to specific bases (put the card on one edge of the base, so you can tell who owns which cards); there may be some immediate effects that trigger. Actions are played like instants or enchantments (i.e., they either do something and go away, or hang around with ongoing effects). After cards have been played, check the sum of all minion strengths on all bases; if sum equals or exceeds the base requirement, score the base(s) (in order of your choice). More abilities may then trigger, after which remaining cards go to players' discard piles; the base is discarded and replaced with a new one from the deck.

If anyone gets to 15+ VP, the game ends and whoever has the most VP wins (if tied, keep playing till there's no tie)! Otherwise, draw 2 cards (if exceed 10-card hand limit, discard down). Finally, end-of-turn effects trigger, then the next player goes.

[b]--- Comments ---[/b]
We only played a partial game to 8 points. 

It's a neat area-majority type of game, but the main draw is going to be exploring the variety of combinations (8 pick 2 = 28 unique pairings) of decks possible in the game. Each deck has a different flair, and it will be fun trying out the different combos. The game play itself is very simple, though. As long as the game plays quickly, it'll probably be fun. I'd guess that this will be better, at least initially, with fewer players; it was difficult tracking what all the cards on all the bases can do at any given time! 

I would like to see:
- a score track: I don't like having to track points on paper
- some way to indicate current totals on bases: didn't like having to repeatedly recalculate all the strengths of cards played on the 5 bases; especially annoying were the cards with one base strength, but a different strength on other players' turns or under specific conditions
- this will probably make for a fun iOS app

RATING: 8

+Smash Up: Awesome Level 9000 (2013) 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This was a bit of an impulse buy looking to get something that was more of a quick filler and easy-to-teach family game.  And it is that for the most part.  With the maximum of four it can play in about 30 minutes (a little longer if players are unfamiliar with the cards and their interactions but faster if they are well-versed) and is very simple to teach although because of the variety of cards and different factions isn't so simple that it gets uninteresting too quickly.

The references and art are goofy fun but the game is a bit more simple than I like.  I've decided to be okay with that though, for the purpose it serves simple is good.

The box calls this a "shufflebuilding" game, which is a new term to me but I suppose it fits.  It's essentially a deck-building game where you don't build a deck.  You get 40 cards at the start of the game (consisting of two 20-card factions) and draw randomly from that deck of cards and play them to fill up sites.  When they fill up they score the majority owners points (so there's a bit of an area control element as well).

Because of the different factions which play very differently it feels a bit like Puzzle Strike but simpler and quicker to play (and a lot quicker to set up and break down).

With expansions this could grow to be one of my favorite fillers and simple family games.

RATING: 8
This game is fun and semi-light once players get familiar with the cards. The first game with any faction will require some reading. 

IMO, this is a 3-player only game. 2-players cause minor balance issues and some cards (especially bases) won't work as well. 4-players bring too much unpredictability and down time. 

A typical player turn typically has 2 or 3 interesting play choices, and in conjunction with board/score analysis, it offers solid gameplay. 

The main issue with the game is the propensity to get hands that are too heavy with either actions or creatures.

RATING: 6.5
The game just dragged on too long for what it is. My wife is also prone to AP when playing  games with varying card effects.  My son and I loved the art work, theme, and the idea of the game.  We didn't enjoy the way it was executed though.

For us the game was  too long and this feeling was increased because so many of the cards cancel out previously played cards.  This just seemed to go on and on.  Ultimately, while it may not be a direct comparison...we much prefer King of Tokyo.

Update:  A couple of years after trading it away my daughter became interested in the game. So...I traded for a copy.  Hope she likes it.

After a couple of plays she wanted to get some expansions and I was  able to pick 3 of them up in a math trade.  She likes the art, combining her factions, and winning bases (and the game as she has won the last two).

My wife played with us tonight and it didn't go so well.  Playing on a Friday after a long week is not the best idea. I also forgot that my wife does not care for games with cards that have different powers/effects.  Smash Up is full of individual card powers, base powers, and you have to keep track of the powers of your opponents cards too.  All of this combined into some prolonged turns for my wife only to have my daughter or I let her know that what she was playing might not be a good idea.  This lead to her having to rethink her turn all over again.  Not a good game for her but I am glad my daughter enjoys it.  

This is the second game that I had traded away earlier and ended up trading for it again.  DC Deck Builder was the first.

RATING: 8
En franÃ§ais
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
After over 50 plays, I feel confident in rating this game (plays include expansions). Many of the reviewers who give low scores miss out on the main strategy of the game-it is not using the cards in a "take-that!" style that is the heart of the strategy, but rather deciding when to use them, when to gamble, and how much to commit.
I think of the game like a war. There are several battlefields (the bases) that you want to win in order to advance your goals in the war (gain victory points). If you commit minions and actions to one base, other players will now have the ability to come in and counter what you're doing, even though you have a jump on them. If you wait, you might be able to swoop in at the last minute, but you have to plan this carefully so you don't miss out. Spreading out your forces can get you a lot of little scores on bases, but rarely any big scores. This battlefield strategy will vary based on the factions that you and your opponents pick. Some factions excel at quick scores, some at spreading around, some at countering, and others at assisting. 
Scoring bases is not the only consideration, as you will generally be scoring more than six bases over the course of the game. The long game is just as important as the immediate gains. Do you decide to get that extra victory point, even though it costs you one of your best cards? Are you willing to solo-score a base, even if it takes a while? Will you concede to another player who is behind you? Sacrifice a potential winning combo on your next turn to prevent another player from potentially winning the whole game? Planning on when to use your cards is absolutely crucial to success (which is a reason Zombies are powerful-ability to reuse cards in discard). The importance of the long game also helps to make the random factor of card draws less of a factor, as players will generally go through at least 3/4 of their decks.
All of this planning, from the faction-picking stage (always fun to find new combinations, it's true) to the individual base battles to the long-game make for intense games and high replay value.
There are some flaws. In the base game alone, zombies, robots, and aliens are very dominant. This is mitigated somewhat by expansions (and house rules, if you so choose), but it's there. Other factions can still win (Pirates actually counter robots well)-it's more like Street Figher 4 balance than Smash Bros. Melee balance. The rules are brief, and with all of the cards (especially with expansions) you are going to run into situations where you have to decide a rule for yourselves. This is typically a very odd situation, but the potential exists (hopefully you have friends who are willing to reasonably discuss how it should be interpreted). Finally, if a game is short, players may feel like they had bad draws (as with most games involving decks).
In my eyes, the war-esque strategy more than makes up for these small flaws. While it is disguised under the "Hey! Look! You can combine robots and laser dinos OMG" promotion of the game, the strategy is very much there, and very fun. Highly recommended.

RATING: 4.2
Pros: It doesn't make me hateful. 

Some of the artwork is pretty good.

Like being stabbed in the heart, it's over quickly(ish).

Cons: This game consists of almost no decisions (it's a small step from Cthulu dice and on a par with Zombie Dice) in that it basically plays itself.

You need to be aware of all of the cards on the table that have ongoing effects/interact with your cards when you play them/the abilities of the HQs. A lot of this will be upside down and far away.

Drawing the right cards, in the right order, at the right time is more important than anything you can do in the game.

Did I mention it basically plays itself?

I'm not entirely sure how a company decide to put this in to production, since the only good thing about it (the art) wouldn't have existed at the prototype phase... Ignore the hype (is there any?), ignore the pretty box, there's basically no game here.

RATING: 7
Smash Up is elegantly simple fun. Undemanding fighting for locations using your servants and sending them to places was here many times before and could be a little old-fashioned, if it were not for one substantial thing. You use combination of two totally different races to play the game and no matter, if it is robots, ninjas or wizards. Each nation has specific abilities, which guarantee very dynamic and unique experience with each shuffling. Match takes place in several locations, which are randomly uncovered and player with most army there gets points, when it reaches an breaking point (power of minions there). But you have no place to mark these numbers, so you have to use pencil and paper and also have to count very often. Whole game contains untraditional graphics and enormous variability, which makes Smash Up to a really interesting game. Lightning fast turns of each player consists of playing a maximum of two cards, while maintaining chance to largely influence enemy outcomes. And this puts Smash Up for one of leaders in a relaxing games genre. Yes some might find, that this game simplifies stuff too much, while others will be thrilled, that it has become game for families and even children. And then there is also a fact, that dominating over cards in nothing new. But if you are just a little tempted by idea of combining races, then you must definitely give Smash Up a try! 

Whole review:
http://www.deskovehry.com/s-pribehem/1000/review-smash-up-when-dinosaurs-zombies-and-aliens-crush/en

RATING: 1
Smash-Up rides extremely heavily on its "NEATO!" factor (heavy breathing optional). You can tell by its regular output of expansions, designed to cash in on the latest, dankest memes. There's some strategy in here, but in tactical execution it basically comes down to who draws the abusive combo in their deck first; the strategic bits are just there to inform you which cards are abusive. Then if you're not that person, you hope to weather the storm or draw your own obnoxious card soon.

If I were to play a game where the underlying premise is to strategically annoy the shit out of one's opponents, it would certainly be one where doing so requires some actual skill instead of random card draw, and also whose presentation weren't so unrepentantly twee.

RATING: 6
Culled - First wave; Gifted to Steve

Why we culled it: Smash Up is an interesting beast. You take an ever-expanding pool of geek tropes and build 20-card decks around them. Players choose any two decks, smash (shuffle) them together, and have a unique combo designed to trigger geek'gasms of awesomeness - Robot zombies? Dinosaur ninjas?! Yeah, and we're just getting started. Smash Up is a great gateway, in that it features content that can excite and engage almost anyone, and it all feels (at least initially) very unique and personalized. 

The problem with Smash Up is that it sacrifices its greatest strength by simply taking too long to resolve. Players LOVE choosing factions and seeing what they do - for the first 15-20 minutes. After that, the hype has subsided, the game starts to get nasty as leaders emerge and are tackled, and everyone is too busy daydreaming about the next exciting combo to focus on the slogfest of the next 30 minutes. Smash Up should be able to play out in 20 minutes, not an hour. If people could return to the faction-selection thrills more often and avoid the often painful swings of the late game, this would be an unstoppable franchise. As it stands, expansions are cashing in on the few niches left, but the game itself remains too long and thus too forgettable. 

I still like the idea of Smash Up, and we have had some fun games. But in the end, we never wanted to play it so much as to just talk about it. "Bear Pirates are awesome! Let's play something else." So I found it a better home.

RATING: 7
Smash up is not a deck-building game, per say.  At the beginning of the round, you combine two different decks and then fight it out with other players.

The game effectively revolves around area control, as you attempt to gain a superior force at the different bases on the table.  As each base succumbs to various forces, a new base is revealed.

I definitely enjoy the game, but it feels a little thin out of the box.  More factions would be great, although for the low cost this is a great multiplayer game that plays quickly.

One of the stronger elements of the game is the artwork and theme of the different decks; they are very diverse.  The production value on this game is very strong, and the manual is very well put together as well.  AEG did a great job with all of the bits.

One last gripe; they need victory point counters!  You need a pen and paper to play, although this obviously isn't the end of the world.  Dice are helpful, as they can help track the strength at a base which is an important mechanic.

**I do not recommend this game for 2 players.   I am in the minority here on the poll, apparently, but there are way better 2 player card games out there.  The "gotcha" nature of Smash Up is really geared towards multiplayer.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I was dubious about this game from the outset.  I'm not a big fan of various themes like zombies, mechadinosaurs pirates, etc.  Even less so at the thought of combining them.  For some the "smash up" of those themes is enough that the game itself wouldn't matter.  So I didn't have a lot of expectations for this one.

But I ended up liking the game a little.  There is some strategy going on and I felt like I was making interesting choices.  

It definitely has the potential to be too "wonky" and probably too random, but that wasn't a big issue in my first play.  I think the biggest drawback to the game is that it was introduced as a light filler, but it ended up inducing a little AP and threatened to drag way past the filler time limit.

RATING: 10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
About [thing=122522][/thing]:

[b]1) What is it?[/b]
[thing=122522][/thing] is a card game in which the player mix two thematic decks to form her own play deck, composed of two different themes, like Zombie Pirates, or Dinosaurs Wizards, for instance.

The goal is use the unusual dual to destroy bases and gather points by doing this. The gameplay for [thing=122522][/thing] is really straightforward: the basic is, in a regular turn, to play a minion and play an action. Yes, all cards have some effect when played, even minions, but the cards are clear enough to leave little doubts about the use of them.

[thing=122522][/thing] truly pits players against each other, as even though there are several bases open to add minions to, naturally players will try to hinder others and get ahead in every point, be by simply taking some opportunity points when adding a single minion to a base, or to flat out destroy or move the minions of someone else, completely changing their objectives and perspectives.

While it is possible to prepare some later plays and build some skeleton of strategy, there is little control in [thing=122522][/thing], as there are so many things that can happen in the other players turns that try to predict what will is simply foolish. The gameplay, therefore, is much more tatical in nature: just do the best you can with the cards you have in hand and considering the state of the game right now.

Overall, [thing=122522][/thing] is a game with a low bar entry: simply mix two decks together and start playing, with very little to teach before the game gets going - the cards will contain the needed information for during play. There is fun to be had playing, but the game can run for too long considering the chaos and tatical gameplay - it would work best as a 30 minute game, not a 50 to 1 hour (and maybe more), and this can hurt deeply its chances of returning to the table often.

[b]2) How do you play?[/b]
Mix two decks. In her turn the player plays: one minion and one action, in the order she chooses. Apply the effects of them.

Check to see if in one or more bases the strength of the minions reached the limit - in this case, the base is destroyed and points are awarded to the players accordingly to what is printed in the base.

If one or more players got to 15 points or more, the game is over. The player with the most points will be the winner!

[b]3) Which are the decisions made during play?[/b]
What cards to play. These are usually in consideration with the game state:
- what bases are in play;
- what is the strength the other players - and you - have in each base;
- what are the points of each player.

That is because you will need to pick a base to add a minion to, and the effect of it can have a greater effect in one more than in other, or the minion in a certain place will allow the use of a nasty action, or is simply a matter of adding strength to be in first/second/third when a base is destroyed and points are awarded - the same is valid for actions, but these are, usually, one shot type of use, while minions remain on play until the base they are in is destroyed. Therefore, the use of actions are even more tatical than minions - still, the decision of which to use and when are mostly an easy affair to decide.

[b]4) What are the good things in the game?[/b]
- Easy to teach and very quick to start playing;
- Highly interactive;
- The different bases and possible mixes make for a good amount of replay value.

[b]5) Which are the bad news?[/b]
- Language dependant - almost all cards contain important text;
- Play can run for too long;
- Can suffer from bash the leader;
- Has lots of take that cards;
- Chaotic.

[b]6) How do you feel while playing?[/b]
Like living in the mind of a very imaginative 10 year old kid.

[thing=122522][/thing] is a design that would leave me frothing from the mouth when I was a pre-teen and I would dedicate months of my life to it, until, well, the next thing that would call for my young and fleeting devotion. Now it just got some smiles when talking out loud about "Ninjas Zombies" and "Aliens Wizards" and, after 1 hour, constants looks to the clock while hoping the game would end soon - but with all the killing of minions and changes of places, it would probably take at least 15 more minutes.

While the gameplay for [thing=122522][/thing] has some merits, it stays in second place to the luck of the draw and the reigning chaos. It is surprising that the factions are balanced, as the end scores usually shows, even though some do go better together than with others, but the margin is low enough for be lost in our also low quality play.

In the end I simply don't see a good enough reason to return to [thing=122522][/thing] with any sort of consistency. It works well for what it is intended to, but it fails to have resonance with me, both in the fun department and in gameplay, as either seems hollow to me, with many better options out there.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4.5
Essentially a majorities game, playing cards with numbered strength to one of several areas, trying to place 1st, 2nd, or 3rd in strength when an area "fills up" with the necessary amount of strength points.

Every player controls two unique factions, making it asymmetrical. The cards have take-that powers to destroy or move or protect other cards, and there's an annoying amount of reading/remembering the text on the cards in play.

The game also seems to want to resemble a deck-builder, with a distinction between one's deck, the cards in hand, and the discard pile. But as you can't alter the composition of your deck, it's a superficial feeling (and BGG doesn't call it a deck-builder at all).

RATING: 5.5
I really like the idea of smashing two factions together into a single deck, but this game has several flaws that I can't ignore.  The game drags because people constantly have to read each card's ability (not only in their hand but also all the cards on the field) to determine the best move. The different combinations are interesting, and it does have a little bit of that TCG feel, but there are just too many special powers going on at the same time to keep track of them all.  Perhaps it would be better with 2 or even 3 players, but as of now, I'm not all that impressed.

RATING: 6
First saw this game on Wil Wheatons Tabletop show. A friend of mine got this game and we've played it a couple of times sense. I really liked this game the first time. I liked it a little bit less the second, and a little bit less with each player after that. This is not a great sign. Basically my favorite part of this game is getting to draft my team and to imagine what they'd be like. ROBOT ALIENS? THAT SOUNDS AWESOME! However, actually playing the game is pretty boring and has so pretty bad Kingbuilding issues similiar to that of Munchkin. 

You play minion cards and event cards to try to win certain territories, however you'll get targeted if you are winning. Most cards are pretty boring and there will be multiple of them in your deck. Some cards are pretty cool though.

I'm fine only playing this on a rare occasion, and mainly just for the laughs of the combination that I make.

RATING: 8

It's just for geeks and nerds with tendency to have their favorite theme/faction battle out for supremacy. (hint: Zombie Ninja's RULES). That being said it's core mechanics are simple but the amount of things you need to consider when playing the game is that it gets slow if you play with people for the first time. Reading takes time people. But once you and your gaming group is finally used to how the game works it's definitely going to be fun filled geek and nerd battle!
